import torch
from diffusers import UNet2DConditionModel


class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.model = UNet2DConditionModel.from_pretrained(self.model_name, subfolder="unet")

    def forward(self, sample, timestep, encoder_hidden_states):
        return self.model(sample, timestep, encoder_hidden_states).sample


model_name = "stabilityai/stable-diffusion-xl-base-1.0"
batch_size = 2
channels = 4
height = 64
width = 64
context_len = 77
cross_attn_dim = 2048


def get_inputs():
    sample = torch.randn(batch_size, channels, height, width)
    timestep = torch.randint(0, 1000, (batch_size,), dtype=torch.long)
    encoder_hidden_states = torch.randn(batch_size, context_len, cross_attn_dim)
    return [sample, timestep, encoder_hidden_states]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name]

We can patch the model to use this fusion. We can write a CUDA kernel that takes an NCHW tensor, computes GroupNorm, applies learnable gamma/beta, and applies SiLU, all in one pass. The kernel would: 1. For each spatial position and group, load elements into registers/shared memory 2. Compute mean and variance for the group 3. For each element: normalize, apply gamma/beta, apply SiLU 4. Write back This avoids writing the GroupNorm output to global memory and reading it back for SiLU. In SDXL UNet, every ResNet block has `norm1` followed by `nonlinearity` (SiLU). There are many such blocks. Fusing them could save significant memory bandwidth. Implementing GroupNorm in CUDA is non-trivial but manageable. We need to handle: - Input: (N, C, H, W) - Groups: typically 32 - Channels: 320, 640, 1280 For each sample and group, we compute mean and variance over C/group * H * W elements. The kernel can use shared memory or warp shuffles for reduction. Since C/group * H * W is at least (320/32)*64*64 = 40*4096 = 163,840 elements, we have plenty of parallelism. Actually, let me think about the grouping in diffusers ResNet blocks. In `ResnetBlock2D`, the GroupNorm typically uses `num_groups=32` and `eps=1e-6` or similar. Wait, but in the Transformer2DModel, the norm is usually LayerNorm, not GroupNorm. LayerNorm is already well fused in PyTorch. So the GroupNorm fusion opportunity is in the ResNet blocks only. In a typical SDXL UNet, how many ResNet blocks are there? - Down blocks: say 3 down blocks with 2 layers each = 6 ResNet blocks, plus maybe more for the cross-attn blocks - Mid block: 2 ResNet blocks - Up blocks: 3 up blocks with 2 layers each = 6 ResNet blocks, plus more Total: perhaps 20-30 ResNet blocks. Each ResNet block has 1-2 GroupNorms. So total GroupNorm calls: ~30-50. Each one reads and writes a tensor of size batch * C * H * W. At smaller resolutions, H*W is smaller (32x32, 16x16, 8x8). The memory bandwidth savings from fusing GroupNorm+SiLU would be: - Without fusion: read tensor (NCHW), compute norm, write tensor (NCHW), read tensor, apply SiLU, write tensor. - With fusion: read tensor, compute norm+SiLU, write tensor. - Savings: one full read/write of the activation. For 64x64x320x2 = ~8.4M parameters, reading+writing fp32 is 67MB per pass. Saving one pass saves ~67MB of memory bandwidth. If the GPU has 1TB/s bandwidth, that's 67 microseconds saved per GroupNorm. With 50 GroupNorms, total savings: ~3.3ms. Is 3.3ms significant? If the model takes 100-500ms, this is only 0.6-3%. Not huge. But if combined with other optimizations, it could help. Actually, I realize there might be an even bigger opportunity. In some diffusers implementations, the attention scores are computed with: ```python attention_scores = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) * self.scale attention_probs = attention_scores.softmax(dim=-1) hidden_states = torch.matmul(attention_probs, value) ``` The intermediate `attention_scores` and `attention_probs` are large tensors. For self-attention with 4096 query and key tokens, and 20 heads, batch 2: the attention matrix is 40 x 4096 x 4096. In fp32, that's 40 * 16M * 4 bytes = 2.56 GB for ONE attention op! But wait, it's usually computed per head or in tiles. Actually, `torch.matmul` for Q@K^T with shapes [40, 4096, 64] @ [40, 64, 4096] produces [40, 4096, 4096]. That's 2.5B elements. For fp32, the output alone is 10 GB. That can't be right for a single attention - actually in UNet, the spatial dimensions are smaller. Wait, the first cross-attention down block might have H=W=64. The channels are 320. For a Transformer2DModel with num_heads=5, head_dim=64, the spatial tokens are 64*64 = 4096. So Q and K for self-attention are [2, 5, 4096, 64]. Matmul gives [2, 5, 4096, 4096]. That's 2 * 5 * 4096 * 4096 = 167,772,160 elements. In fp32, 671 MB. For one attention layer! If there are multiple self-attention layers (say 10 in the mid block), and if they all do this, the memory is being written and read heavily. But actually, PyTorch matmul doesn't necessarily materialize all of this at once if using certain algorithms. Wait, batched matmul of [2, 5, 4096, 64] @ [2, 5, 64, 4096] is 2*5 = 10 independent matmuls of [4096, 64] @ [64, 4096]. cuBLAS handles this. The output is indeed [2, 5, 4096, 4096], which is 671 MB. And then softmax is applied, and then another matmul with value. So the peak memory for this single op is ~1 GB. The bandwidth to write and read this is significant. Flash Attention reduces this by keeping the intermediate results in SRAM and computing the output without writing the full attention matrix to HBM. This is why it can be 2-4x faster and more memory efficient. If diffusers is NOT using Flash Attention / SDPA, then enabling SDPA would be a massive win. For a UNet with ~20 ResNet blocks, we'd need ~20 custom kernels, each handling specific dimensions. In practice, there are only a few unique configurations (e.g., channel counts repeat). So maybe 6-10 unique kernels. But writing even one such kernel is hundreds of lines of CUDA. And then we need to handle loading weights, applying convolutions (which requires calling cuDNN or writing conv kernels - impossible), etc. So this doesn't work because convolutions are too complex. Unless... we call cuDNN from our CUDA kernel? That's possible but requires linking against cuDNN and is very complex. So fusing at the ResNet block level is not feasible. What about fusing just within the Transformer block? The main operations are: - LayerNorm - Linear (Q, K, V projections) - Attention (SDPA) - Linear (out projection) - LayerNorm - FFN (Linear + GELU + Linear) - Residual adds These are all highly optimized individually. Fusing them would require writing kernels for linear layers (matmuls), which we can't beat cuBLAS on. Given all these considerations, I believe the most practical winning strategy is: **Use SDPA for attention + torch.compile for graph optimization + minimal custom kernel** Actually, I'll just use `torch.compile`. Actually, `get_timestep_embedding` is defined in diffusers and does: ```python def get_timestep_embedding(timesteps, embedding_dim, flip_sin_to_cos=False, downscale_freq_shift=0, scale=1, max_period=10000): half_dim = embedding_dim // 2 exponent = -math.log(max_period) * torch.arange(...).to(timesteps.device) emb = timesteps[:, None].float() * emb[None, :] emb = torch.cat([torch.sin(emb), torch.cos(emb)], dim=-1) if flip_sin_to_cos: emb = torch.cat([emb[:, half_dim:], emb[:, :half_dim]], dim=-1) return emb * scale ``` This is a simple element-wise computation. This kernel is dead simple but it fuses add + silu, which saves one memory round-trip. Where can I use it? In the time embedding path or in the MLP of transformer blocks. Actually, let me reconsider the channels_last idea. For convolutions on H100 with Tensor Cores, channels_last (NHWC) is the optimal layout. The reference model probably runs in NCHW. Let me write this up properly. I'll also try to implement a custom GroupNorm+SiLU kernel as a proof of custom CUDA usage. For the GroupNorm kernel, I need to compute: 1. Mean and variance per group 2. Normalize 3. Scale and shift 4. Apply SiLU The kernel can be implemented with shared memory for reduction. Let me draft the CUDA code: ```cuda __global__ void groupnorm_silu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int total_elements = N * C * H * W; int elements_per_group = C / num_groups * H * W; // Each block handles one sample's one group int ng = N * num_groups; int block_stride = gridDim.x; for (int idx = blockIdx.x; idx < ng; idx += block_stride) { int n = idx / num_groups; int g = idx % num_groups; int c_start = g * (C / num_groups); int c_end = c_start + (C / num_groups); // Compute mean float sum = 0.0f; for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; ++c) { for (int h = threadIdx.x; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { for (int w = 0; w < W; ++w) { int offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; sum += input[offset]; } } } // Warp reduction + shared memory reduction for sum __shared__ float shared_mean; __shared__ float shared_var; // ... reduction ... float mean = sum / elements_per_group; // Compute variance float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; ++c) { for (int h = threadIdx.x; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { for (int w = 0; w < W; ++w) { int offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; float diff = input[offset] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } } } // ... reduction ... float var = sq_sum / elements_per_group; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize, scale, shift, SiLU for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; ++c) { float w = weight[c]; float b = bias[c]; for (int h = threadIdx.x; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { for (int w_idx = 0; w_idx < W; ++w_idx) { int offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w_idx; float norm = (input[offset] - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * w + b; // SiLU output[offset] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } } } } ``` This is a basic version but should work for the relatively small sizes in UNet (N=2, C=320/640/1280, H/W from 64 down to 8). Wait, I realize that a naive implementation like this might not be faster than PyTorch's optimized GroupNorm + SiLU. PyTorch's GroupNorm uses highly optimized reduction kernels. Here's how diffusers ResnetBlock2D forward works (approximately): ```python def forward(self, input_tensor, temb): hidden_states = input_tensor hidden_states = self.norm1(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv1(hidden_states) if temb is not None: temb = self.time_emb_proj(self.nonlinearity(temb)) hidden_states = hidden_states + temb[:, :, None, None] hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv2(hidden_states) if self.conv_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.conv_shortcut(input_tensor) output_tensor = (input_tensor + hidden_states) / self.output_scale_factor return output_tensor ``` If I can fuse `norm1 + nonlinearity` and `norm2 + nonlinearity`, I save two element-wise passes. My custom `groupnorm_silu` kernel takes `weight` and `bias` buffers. Inside `fast_forward`, `self.norm1` is a `nn.GroupNorm` object. I can access `self.norm1.weight` and `self.norm1.bias`. But a `nn.GroupNorm` already has optimized CUDA kernels. My custom kernel needs to be at least as fast and also include SiLU to be worth it. Actually, the real savings isn't in the computation itself - it's in fusing GroupNorm and SiLU into a single kernel, eliminating the write-to-memory and read-back of the intermediate tensor. For memory-bound workloads, this matters. Let me write the fused kernel properly. I'll use a two-pass approach: first compute mean and variance, then normalize and apply SiLU. Here's a better kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void groupnorm_silu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int n = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int spatial_size = H * W; int elements_per_group = group_size * spatial_size; extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* s_mean = shared; float* s_var = &shared[1]; // Compute mean for this group float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = g * group_size + threadIdx.x; c < (g + 1) * group_size; c += blockDim.x) { for (int hw = 0; hw < spatial_size; ++hw) { int idx = ((n * C + c) * H * W) + hw; local_sum += input[idx]; } } // Reduce local_sum across threads __syncthreads(); // ... warp reduction ... if (threadIdx.x == 0) { *s_mean = local_sum / elements_per_group; } __syncthreads(); float mean = *s_mean; // Compute variance float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = g * group_size + threadIdx.x; c < (g + 1) * group_size; c += blockDim.x) { for (int hw = 0; hw < spatial_size; ++hw) { int idx = ((n * C + c) * H * W) + hw; float diff = input[idx] - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } } // Reduce // ... if (threadIdx.x == 0) { *s_var = local_sq_sum / elements_per_group; } __syncthreads(); float var = *s_var; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Apply normalization, scale, bias, and SiLU for (int c = g * group_size + threadIdx.x; c < (g + 1) * group_size; c += blockDim.x) { float w = weight[c]; float b = bias[c]; for (int hw = 0; hw < spatial_size; ++hw) { int idx = ((n * C + c) * H * W) + hw; float norm = (input[idx] - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * w + b; output[idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } } ``` This kernel processes one group per block. With shared memory for mean and variance. For N=2, C=320, H=W=64, num_groups=32: - There are 2*32 = 64 groups - Each group has 320/32 = 10 channels and 4096 spatial locations - elements_per_group = 10*4096 = 40960 With 256 threads per block, each thread handles about 160 elements. This is reasonable. For smaller resolutions (H=W=8), it's 10*64 = 640 elements per group, so each thread handles ~2.5 elements. That's still fine. Now, I need to handle the reduction properly. Let me write a cleaner version using warp shuffles. Actually, instead of trying to write an optimal reduction from scratch, let me use a simpler approach: each thread computes its local sum, then we do a parallel reduction. Or even simpler: for the sizes involved, I can use atomicAdd to accumulate sums. But atomicAdd to shared memory is fast enough. ```cuda // Shared storage __shared__ float shared_sum[256]; __shared__ float shared_sq_sum[256]; shared_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; shared_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { shared_sum[threadIdx.x] += shared_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; shared_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] += shared_sq_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { mean = shared_sum[0] / elements_per_group; var = shared_sq_sum[0] / elements_per_group; } ``` This is a standard tree reduction. It assumes blockDim.x is a power of 2. I can use 128 or 256. But wait, the local_sum and local_sq_sum are computed in two loops. If I compute them in one pass, I save some memory reads. But for simplicity, I'll do two passes. Actually, for better performance, I can compute sum and sum-of-squares in a single pass: ```cuda float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[idx]; local_sum += val; // Can't compute sq_sum without mean... } ``` Actually I need the mean first before computing variance. So two passes are unavoidable. I can also do an online variance computation (Welford's algorithm) in one pass, but it's numerically more complex. Let me just write the kernel and see. Also, I should handle the case where `blockDim.x` isn't a power of 2 by padding or using a more flexible reduction. Another optimization: instead of having each thread loop over the last dimension, I can use 2D thread blocks. But for simplicity, 1D blocks are fine. Let me also think about memory coalescing. For NCHW format, elements along the W dimension are contiguous. So looping over W first gives coalesced access. My kernel loops: ```cuda for (int c = ...) { for (int hw = ...) { int idx = ((n * C + c) * H * W) + hw; } } ``` If `hw` goes from 0 to H*W-1, then adjacent threads (with same c) access adjacent memory. But threads in the same block might handle different c values, so the access pattern depends on the thread indexing. If I do: ```cuda for (int c = g * group_size + threadIdx.x; c < (g + 1) * group_size; c += blockDim.x) { for (int hw = threadIdx.y; hw < spatial_size; hw += blockDim.y) { ``` Then with 2D blocks, I can have better coalescing. Let me use 2D blocks or at least ensure that consecutive threads access consecutive memory. With 1D blocks of size 256: - thread 0 accesses c = g*gs + 0, hw = 0 - thread 1 accesses c = g*gs + 1, hw = 0 These are NOT contiguous in memory! Because the stride between channels is H*W. For NCHW, memory layout is: [N, C, H, W]. Elements with the same spatial location but adjacent channels are H*W apart. For coalesced access, threads should access adjacent elements, which means they should traverse the W (or H*W) dimension with the same channel. So: ```cuda int hw_start = threadIdx.x; // or some partitioning for (int c_start = 0; c_start < group_size; c_start += some_stride) { int c = g * group_size + c_start; for (int hw = threadIdx.x; hw < spatial_size; hw += blockDim.x) { int idx = ((n * C + c) * H * W) + hw; } } ``` Here, consecutive threads access consecutive `hw` values, which are contiguous. That's coalesced. But different threads still access different `hw` values, and we need to reduce across all of them. Let me design the kernel with this access pattern. For each group: - Partition `spatial_size * group_size` elements across threads - Each thread handles a subset - Compute per-thread sum and sum-of-squares - Reduce Actually, a cleaner design: - Each block handles one group - Each thread handles `spatial_size / blockDim.x` spatial locations for one channel, or some 2D mapping Let me just write a working kernel and not over-optimize. PyTorch's built-in kernels are very optimized, but my kernel also fuses SiLU, so even if it's not as efficient, the fusion might help. Wait, actually for a small model like this, and with `torch.compile` potentially fusing these operations anyway, my custom kernel might not be necessary for speed. For simplicity, let me write a kernel for the time embedding addition in ResNet blocks. The pattern is: ```python hidden_states = hidden_states + temb[:, :, None, None] ``` This is a broadcast addition. PyTorch handles it, but I can write a kernel that does it in a fused way with whatever follows. In many ResNet blocks, after adding the time embedding, the next operation is GroupNorm. So if I could fuse `add + groupnorm`, that would be useful. But GroupNorm needs reduction across the group, so fusing with an element-wise add is still possible (just add before reduction). Actually, `add` commutes with mean and variance in a specific way: If `z = x + c` where c is constant per channel, then: - mean(z) = mean(x) + c - var(z) = var(x) So theoretically, we can fuse broadcast add + GroupNorm + SiLU into one kernel. This would be very useful! In ResNet blocks: ```python hidden_states = hidden_states + temb[:, :, None, None] hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) ``` Can be fused as: ```python hidden_states = fused_add_groupnorm_silu(hidden_states, temb, norm_weight, norm_bias, ...) ``` This saves one full read/write of `hidden_states` and one kernel launch. Let me implement this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_groupnorm_silu_kernel( const float* input, const float* temb, float* output, const float* weight, const float* bias, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int n = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int spatial = H * W; int total_elements = group_size * spatial; __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_mean; __shared__ float s_inv_std; // Each thread computes local sum over its assigned elements float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = ((n * C + c) * spatial) + hw; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; local_sum += val; local_sq_sum += val * val; } s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); // Reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float mean = s_sum[0] / total_elements; float sq_mean = s_sq_sum[0] / total_elements; float var = sq_mean - mean * mean; s_mean = mean; s_inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean; float inv_std = s_inv_std; // Apply normalization, scale, bias, SiLU for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = ((n * C + c) * spatial) + hw; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[flat_idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } ``` Wait, variance computation as `E[x^2] - E[x]^2` can suffer from catastrophic cancellation. For robustness, I should use the two-pass method (compute mean first, then variance). But for a fused kernel, the two-pass approach requires either storing intermediate values or doing two loops. With shared memory, I can store the mean after the first pass and compute variance in the second pass. This is what I was doing before. Actually, for the element count in each group (e.g., 10*4096 = 40960 for 64x64), floating point accumulation of sum-of-squares is fine. The cancellation issue is minor for typical UNet activations. But let me be safe and use the two-pass approach. ```cuda // First pass: compute mean float local_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; local_sum += val; } // ... reduce to mean ... // Second pass: compute variance float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float diff = val - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } // ... reduce to var ... ``` This requires two loops over the data, which doubles the memory reads. But the memory reads are coalesced and we avoid writing an intermediate tensor to global memory. So overall it's probably still a win. For large NCHW where memory bandwidth is the bottleneck, fusing might save enough to compensate. But there's another issue: `temb` is added as `temb[:, :, None, None]`, which means for each sample n and channel c, the same value is added to all spatial locations. In my kernel, `temb[n * C + c]` assumes `temb` is flattened as [N*C]. That's correct. Now, the tricky part is integrating this kernel. For a robust monkey-patch, I need to handle: 1. `temb is None`: skip the add and just do GroupNorm + SiLU 2. `output_scale_factor != 1`: division happens after conv2 3. `conv_shortcut`: needs to be applied to input_tensor 4. `dropout`: after nonlinearity in the second block My fused kernel only replaces `add + norm2 + nonlinearity`. But then what does my custom kernel do? Option 1: Keep NCHW. My custom kernel works. But I lose the speedup from channels_last. Option 2: Adapt my kernel for channels_last. The kernel code changes to use the correct indexing. Let me do Option 2. I'll adapt the kernel for channels_last layout. For channels_last, indexing is: ```cuda int flat_idx = ((n * H + h) * W + w) * C + c; ``` Or equivalently: ```cuda int flat_idx = n * C * H * W + h * W * C + w * C + c; ``` For the spatial loop where I iterate over hw (from 0 to H*W-1): ```cuda int h = hw / W; int w = hw % W; int flat_idx = ((n * H + h) * W + w) * C + c; ``` Or even better, since channels_last has stride[C] = 1, stride[W] = C, stride[H] = W*C, stride[N] = H*W*C: ```cuda int flat_idx = n * H * W * C + h * W * C + w * C + c; ``` I'll write the kernel to accept a `channels_last` flag and adjust indexing accordingly. Actually, wait. If I use channels_last, does `nn.GroupNorm` work correctly? Yes, PyTorch's GroupNorm handles channels_last. Does `nn.Conv2d` with channels_last get accelerated by cudnn? Yes, on Ampere+. So converting to channels_last is definitely beneficial. For my custom kernel, I need to properly index the channels_last tensor. I'll write the kernel generically. But wait - for the kernel to work with the monkey-patched ResNet blocks, all the PyTorch operations in the block (conv, etc.) need to work with channels_last. They do. And my custom kernel will operate on a channels_last tensor. So I need to use the correct indexing. Let me rewrite the kernel for channels_last: ```cuda // channels_last indexing // tensor[n, c, h, w] is at: n * (H*W*C) + h * (W*C) + w * C + c // flattening over h,w: for hw in [0, H*W): // h = hw / W, w = hw % W // idx = n * H*W*C + h * W*C + w * C + c // = n * H*W*C + hw * C + c int flat_idx = n * H * W * C + hw * C + c; ``` This is simple! `hw` ranges over 0..H*W-1, and for each hw, the channels are contiguous. So the kernel for channels_last is actually easier: ```cuda int flat_idx = n * spatial * C + hw * C + c; ``` where `spatial = H * W`. Wait, that assumes C channels are contiguous starting at `base + hw*C`. For channels_last, yes! Because channels_last has stride for C = 1. But is `hw*C` the correct offset from `n * spatial * C`? For n=0, h=0, w=0: idx = 0 + 0 + 0 + c = c. Correct. For n=0, h=0, w=1: idx = 0 + 0 + 1*C + c = C + c. Correct. For n=0, h=1, w=0: idx = 0 + 1*W*C + 0 + c = W*C + c. Correct. Yes! For channels_last, `idx = (n * spatial + hw) * C + c`. For NCHW, `idx = (n * C + c) * spatial + hw`. Great, my kernel can handle both by checking a flag or using a template. For simplicity, I'll just support channels_last since that's what I plan to use. Now let me also think about the `temb` tensor. `temb` has shape [N, C] and is typically contiguous. When we add `temb[:, :, None, None]` to a channels_last tensor, PyTorch broadcasts accordingly. In my kernel, `temb[n * C + c]` is the value for sample n, channel c. Correct. Alright, now let me think about implementing the kernel. One issue with my kernel design: it uses `blockDim.x` threads and partitions `total_elements = group_size * spatial` across them. But `total_elements` might not be evenly divisible by `blockDim.x`. I should handle the reduction carefully. Using a tree reduction requires `blockDim.x` to be a power of 2. I'll use 128 or 256 threads. For the loop: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { ``` Each thread handles a subset of elements. At the end, we reduce the partial sums. For the reduction: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[256]; s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This only works correctly if all threads have valid data. Since we initialized `s_sum[threadIdx.x]` for each thread, it's fine even if some threads didn't contribute to `local_sum` in the loop (they just initialized to 0.0f). Wait, every thread runs the loop `for (idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x)`. If `threadIdx.x >= total_elements`, the loop doesn't execute and `local_sum` remains 0.0f. That's fine. But what if `total_elements < blockDim.x`? Then only `total_elements` threads have non-zero `local_sum`. The rest have 0. The reduction still works because 0 + x = x. Great. Now, for the mean/variance computation, I need to be careful: ```cuda float mean = s_sum[0] / total_elements; float var = s_sq_sum[0] / total_elements; ``` Wait, `s_sq_sum` accumulates `(val - mean)^2`, not `val^2`. I DO need the two-pass approach for accurate variance. Let me structure the kernel: ```cuda // First pass: compute sum float local_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; local_sum += val; } s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Reduce for (...) { ... } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_sum[0] / total_elements; // Second pass: compute sq_diff sum float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; float diff = val - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); // Reduce for (...) { ... } __syncthreads(); float var = s_sq_sum[0] / total_elements; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Third pass: write output for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[...] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } ``` This requires THREE passes over the data! That's a lot. For memory bandwidth, it's still probably fine because we're reading the input once per pass, but each pass goes through memory. Actually, we can combine the first and second passes if we: 1. Store `val` for each element in shared memory? No, shared memory is only 48KB per SM on H100. With C=1280, group_size=40, spatial=4096, total_elements=163840 floats = 655KB. Too big for shared memory. We can store per-thread partial sums and then compute mean, then do a second pass for variance, then a third pass for output. As above. Is there a one-pass algorithm? Welford's online algorithm can compute mean and variance in one pass: ```cuda float mean = 0.0f; float M2 = 0.0f; int count = 0; for each val: count++; float delta = val - mean; mean += delta / count; float delta2 = val - mean; M2 += delta * delta2; var = M2 / count; ``` This requires storing `mean` and `M2` per thread, which is fine. Then after the first pass, we reduce to get the overall mean and M2. Then we need a second pass to write the output. So total: 2 passes over data. Much better! Welford's algorithm is numerically stable and works well. ```cuda // First pass: Welford's algorithm float local_mean = 0.0f; float local_M2 = 0.0f; int local_count = 0; for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; local_count++; float delta = val - local_mean; local_mean += delta / local_count; float delta2 = val - local_mean; local_M2 += delta * delta2; } // Now reduce mean and M2 across threads. // This is more complex than simple sum reduction. // We need to merge Welford states from different threads. ``` Merging Welford states: ```python def merge(mean_a, M2_a, count_a, mean_b, M2_b, count_b): count = count_a + count_b delta = mean_b - mean_a mean = mean_a + delta * count_b / count M2 = M2_a + M2_b + delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count return mean, M2, count ``` This can be done in a tree reduction. ```cuda __shared__ float s_mean[256]; __shared__ float s_M2[256]; __shared__ int s_count[256]; s_mean[threadIdx.x] = local_mean; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = local_M2; s_count[threadIdx.x] = local_count; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { int count_a = s_count[threadIdx.x]; int count_b = s_count[threadIdx.x + stride]; if (count_b > 0) { float mean_a = s_mean[threadIdx.x]; float mean_b = s_mean[threadIdx.x + stride]; float M2_a = s_M2[threadIdx.x]; float M2_b = s_M2[threadIdx.x + stride]; float delta = mean_b - mean_a; int count = count_a + count_b; s_mean[threadIdx.x] = mean_a + delta * count_b / count; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = M2_a + M2_b + delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count; s_count[threadIdx.x] = count; } } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_mean[0]; float var = s_M2[0] / s_count[0]; ``` This is elegant but might have precision issues when merging across threads. However, for our purposes, it should be fine. After the first pass, we have `mean` and `var`. Then we need a second pass to compute and write the output: ```cuda float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[...] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } ``` Only 2 passes! This is good. Let me refine the Welford merge. The formula `delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count` can overflow? Probably not for our ranges. Also, `count_b` could be 0 (if a thread had no elements to process). I handled that with `if (count_b > 0)`. One more thing: `s_count[threadIdx.x + stride]` might be 0, and we skip the merge in that case. OK, let me write the actual CUDA code now. Wait, I just remembered something. For GroupNorm, the group size `C / num_groups` might not be an integer if C is not divisible by num_groups. In practice, it is divisible. e.g., C=320, num_groups=32 -> group_size=10. Integer. Good. Let me also think about the launch configuration. For each group, I need one CUDA block. With N samples and num_groups groups, total blocks = N * num_groups. For a ResNet block in SDXL: - N=2 - num_groups=32 - Total blocks = 64 I can launch 64 blocks of 256 threads each. That's very few blocks for an H100! H100 has 132 SMs, each can run multiple blocks. So 64 blocks might not fully saturate the GPU, but each block has a lot of work. At the lowest resolution (H=W=8), total_elements = group_size * 64. For group_size=10, that's 640 elements per group. With 256 threads, about 2-3 elements per thread. Not much work. But this is the tail end of the UNet. At the highest resolution (H=W=64), total_elements = group_size * 4096. For group_size=10, that's 40960 elements per group. With 256 threads, about 160 elements per thread. Good. For larger channels in deeper layers (C=1280, group_size=40), total_elements = 40 * 64 = 2560 at 8x8, and 40 * 4096 = 163840 at 64x64. With 256 threads, that's 640 elements per thread! That's great. So the kernel should be well-utilized for the large feature maps. Let me now write a complete code. I'll use Welford's algorithm in shared memory reduction. One concern: shared memory size. I need `float s_mean[256]`, `float s_M2[256]`, `int s_count[256]`. Total = 256*(4+4+4) = 3072 bytes per block. Well under the limit. Now, let me draft the complete CUDA code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ temb, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int n = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int spatial = H * W; int total_elements = group_size * spatial; __shared__ float s_mean[256]; __shared__ float s_M2[256]; __shared__ int s_count[256]; // Welford's online algorithm per thread float local_mean = 0.0f; float local_M2 = 0.0f; int local_count = 0; int base_offset = n * spatial * C; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; local_count++; float delta = val - local_mean; local_mean += delta / local_count; float delta2 = val - local_mean; local_M2 += delta * delta2; } s_mean[threadIdx.x] = local_mean; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = local_M2; s_count[threadIdx.x] = local_count; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for Welford for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { int count_a = s_count[threadIdx.x]; int count_b = s_count[threadIdx.x + stride]; if (count_b > 0) { if (count_a == 0) { s_mean[threadIdx.x] = s_mean[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = s_M2[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_count[threadIdx.x] = count_b; } else { float mean_a = s_mean[threadIdx.x]; float mean_b = s_mean[threadIdx.x + stride]; float M2_a = s_M2[threadIdx.x]; float M2_b = s_M2[threadIdx.x + stride]; float delta = mean_b - mean_a; int count = count_a + count_b; s_mean[threadIdx.x] = mean_a + delta * count_b / count; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = M2_a + M2_b + delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count; s_count[threadIdx.x] = count; } } } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_mean[0]; float var = (s_count[0] > 1) ? (s_M2[0] / s_count[0]) : 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Apply normalization, weight, bias, SiLU for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[flat_idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } torch::Tensor fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor temb, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int num_groups, float eps) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); dim3 blocks(num_groups, N); int threads = 256; fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), temb.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, num_groups, eps ); return output; } ``` Wait, I need to handle inputs that might not be float32. The reference uses fp32, so I'll assume float32. Also, `input.size(1)` is the channel dimension. For channels_last tensors, the `.size()` still reports the logical shape [N, C, H, W]. Good. Let me also add a `fused_groupnorm_silu_cl` variant that doesn't add `temb`. ```cuda __global__ void fused_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel(...) { // Same but without temb addition } torch::Tensor fused_groupnorm_silu_cl(...) { ... } ``` Now, for the model optimization, let me think about what else I can do. 1. Channels_last: yes 2. Custom kernel for ResNet blocks: yes 3. Maybe also optimize attention blocks For attention, I can try to replace the attention processor with a custom one that uses a more efficient kernel. But on H100, SDPA should already be optimal. A custom kernel that loads all 77 keys/values into shared memory and processes queries in parallel could be very efficient. For cross-attention: Q shape is [B*heads, H*W, head_dim], K/V shape is [B*heads, 77, head_dim]. For each query token, I need to compute dot product with 77 keys (length 77). I can load K and V for all 77 tokens into shared memory and compute in one shot. ```cuda __shared__ float K_shared[77 * head_dim]; __shared__ float V_shared[77 * head_dim]; ``` For head_dim=40, that's 77*40 = 3080 floats = 12.3KB. For example, if heads=10 and head_dim=64, that's [20, H*W, 64]. The attention score is Q @ K^T: [20, H*W, 77]. This is a batched matrix multiply. For H*W=4096, this is a [20, 4096, 77] matmul. Common attributes of `ResnetBlock2D`: - `self.norm1`: GroupNorm (or similar) - `self.conv1`: Conv2d - `self.time_emb_proj`: Linear (or None) - `self.norm2`: GroupNorm (or similar) - `self.dropout`: Dropout (or None) - `self.conv2`: Conv2d - `self.conv_shortcut`: Conv2d (or None) - `self.nonlinearity`: SiLU (or similar) - `self.output_scale_factor`: float My monkey-patched forward: ```python def fast_resnet_forward(self, input_tensor, temb=None): hidden_states = input_tensor # First groupnorm+conv hidden_states = self.norm1(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv1(hidden_states) # Time embedding if temb is not None and hasattr(self, 'time_emb_proj') and self.time_emb_proj is not None: temb_input = self.nonlinearity(temb) temb_input = self.time_emb_proj(temb_input) temb_input = temb_input[:, :, None, None] else: temb_input = None # Second block: use custom kernel for norm2 + nonlinearity (+ add if temb exists) if temb_input is not None: # Need to handle case where norm2 is not GroupNorm if isinstance(self.norm2, torch.nn.GroupNorm): hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, temb_input, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: hidden_states = hidden_states + temb_input hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) else: if isinstance(self.norm2, torch.nn.GroupNorm): hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) # Dropout if hasattr(self, 'dropout') and self.dropout is not None: hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv2(hidden_states) # Shortcut if hasattr(self, 'conv_shortcut') and self.conv_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.conv_shortcut(input_tensor) elif hasattr(self, 'conv1') and self.conv1.in_channels != self.conv1.out_channels: # Sometimes shortcut is handled differently pass # Final add and scale output_tensor = (input_tensor + hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'output_scale_factor') and self.output_scale_factor != 1.0: output_tensor = output_tensor / self.output_scale_factor return output_tensor ``` Wait, the shortcut handling might be incorrect. Let me write a robust wrapper. ```python def make_fast_resnet_forward(orig_forward): def fast_forward(self, input_tensor, temb=None): # Get the original code but with norm2+nonlinearity fused hidden_states = input_tensor # norm1 + nonlinearity + conv1 hidden_states = self.norm1(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'upsample') and self.upsample is not None: input_tensor = self.upsample(input_tensor) hidden_states = self.upsample(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv1(hidden_states) # time embedding if temb is not None and hasattr(self, 'time_emb_proj') and self.time_emb_proj is not None: temb_input = self.time_emb_proj(self.nonlinearity(temb)) temb_input = temb_input[:, :, None, None] else: temb_input = None # FUSE: add + norm2 + nonlinearity if isinstance(self.norm2, torch.nn.GroupNorm): if temb_input is not None: hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, temb_input, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: # Fallback to original behavior if temb_input is not None: hidden_states = hidden_states + temb_input hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) # downsample if hasattr(self, 'downsample') and self.downsample is not None: input_tensor = self.downsample(input_tensor) hidden_states = self.downsample(hidden_states) # My `fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl` kernel computes GroupNorm + SiLU. The standard PyTorch implementation does exactly the same operations, so the result should be identical up to floating point precision. But there are potential differences: 1. My Welford algorithm for mean/variance might have slightly different precision than PyTorch's GroupNorm 2. The reduction order is different (tree reduction in my kernel vs whatever PyTorch uses) For fp32 with tolerance 1e-4, small differences in reduction order should be fine. Wait, one more thing. PyTorch's GroupNorm uses a highly optimized two-pass algorithm with parallel reductions. My kernel uses Welford's algorithm. Both are correct for fp32. But there's a subtle issue: when I compute the mean, if `total_elements = group_size * H * W` is large, the floating point error in my reduction might differ from PyTorch's. For the largest case (group_size=40, H*W=4096, total=163840), summing 163k values in fp32 has some error but should be within tolerance. PyTorch uses optimized reduction kernels that are well-tested. Also, my mean is computed as a tree reduction. The order of summation might differ from PyTorch's. But for positive numbers (which activations typically are after SiLU... wait, GroupNorm is BEFORE SiLU), the error should be small. Actually, activations in a UNet can be negative too, since they come from previous layers without activation. GroupNorm normalizes them. Reduction error should still be small. To be extra safe, I can use Kahan summation in my kernel, but that's overkill and might slow things down. Let me just use simple summation and hope it's within 1e-4. For SiLU, the computation `x / (1.0 + expf(-x))` might differ slightly from PyTorch's implementation. PyTorch uses different approximation strategies for `exp`. But on GPU, both call the same CUDA intrinsic `__expf` or `expf`. So they should match closely. Alright, let me write the actual code now. Here's the structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline import types # CUDA code cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel(...) { ... } __global__ void fused_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl(...) { ... } torch::Tensor fused_groupnorm_silu_cl(...) { ... } """ cuda_module = load_inline( name="unet_fused_ops", cpp_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl", "fused_groupnorm_silu_cl"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) def patch_resnet_blocks(model): from diffusers.models.resnet import ResnetBlock2D def fast_forward(self, input_tensor, temb=None): hidden_states = input_tensor # First half hidden_states = self.norm1(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'upsample') and self.upsample is not None: input_tensor = self.upsample(input_tensor) hidden_states = self.upsample(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv1(hidden_states) # Time embedding temb_input = None if temb is not None and hasattr(self, 'time_emb_proj') and self.time_emb_proj is not None: temb_input = self.time_emb_proj(self.nonlinearity(temb)) temb_input = temb_input[:, :, None, None] # FUSE second half is_groupnorm = hasattr(self, 'norm2') and isinstance(self.norm2, nn.GroupNorm) if is_groupnorm: if temb_input is not None: hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, temb_input, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: if temb_input is not None: hidden_states = hidden_states + temb_input if hasattr(self, 'norm2') and self.norm2 is not None: hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'nonlinearity') and self.nonlinearity is not None: hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) # Downsample if hasattr(self, 'downsample') and self.downsample is not None: input_tensor = self.downsample(input_tensor) hidden_states = self.downsample(hidden_states) # Conv2 and shortcut if hasattr(self, 'dropout') and self.dropout is not None: hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv2(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'conv_shortcut') and self.conv_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.conv_shortcut(input_tensor) elif hasattr(self, 'nin_shortcut') and self.nin_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.nin_shortcut(input_tensor) scale = getattr(self, 'output_scale_factor', 1.0) output_tensor = (input_tensor + hidden_states) / scale return output_tensor # Apply to all instances count = 0 for module in model.modules(): if isinstance(module, ResnetBlock2D): module.forward = types.MethodType(fast_forward, module) count += 1 class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name): super().__init__() from diffusers import UNet2DConditionModel from diffusers.models.attention_processor import AttnProcessor2_0 torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark = True self.model = UNet2DConditionModel.from_pretrained(model_name, subfolder="unet") # Set efficient attention try: self.model.set_attn_processor(AttnProcessor2_0()) except: pass # Patch ResNet blocks try: patch_resnet_blocks(self.model) except: pass # Convert to channels_last self.model = self.model.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) # Try torch.compile try: self.model = torch.compile(self.model, mode="max-autotune") except: pass def forward(self, sample, timestep, encoder_hidden_states): sample = sample.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) with torch.inference_mode(): out = self.model(sample, timestep, encoder_hidden_states).sample return out.to(memory_format=torch.contiguous_format) ``` Wait, I just realized that `torch.compile` might duplicate my monkey-patched methods. Actually, `torch.compile` traces the Python bytecode, so if I monkey-patch `.forward` on instances, compile might not see the patches because it traces the class methods. No, `torch.compile` traces the actual callable. If `module.forward` is patched, compile traces the patched version. But when compile caches or recompiles, it might cache based on the original class. Actually, `torch.compile` uses the `__call__` method, which dispatches to `forward`. If the instance's `forward` is monkey-patched, `__call__` calls the patched `forward`. So compile should see it. But there can be issues with `torch.compile` caching and monkey-patching. I'll use `fullgraph=False` to be safe. Also, I realize that converting to channels_last affects ALL tensors in the model, including weight tensors. The monkey-patched kernel operates on channels_last tensors, which is what I intended. Let me now think about how to write the Welford reduction more carefully. ```cuda __shared__ float s_mean[256]; __shared__ float s_M2[256]; __shared__ int s_count[256]; s_mean[threadIdx.x] = local_mean; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = local_M2; s_count[threadIdx.x] = local_count; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { int count_a = s_count[threadIdx.x]; int count_b = s_count[threadIdx.x + stride]; if (count_b > 0) { if (count_a == 0) { s_mean[threadIdx.x] = s_mean[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = s_M2[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_count[threadIdx.x] = count_b; } else { float mean_a = s_mean[threadIdx.x]; float mean_b = s_mean[threadIdx.x + stride]; float M2_a = s_M2[threadIdx.x]; float M2_b = s_M2[threadIdx.x + stride]; float delta = mean_b - mean_a; int count = count_a + count_b; s_mean[threadIdx.x] = mean_a + delta * count_b / count; s_M2[threadIdx.x] = M2_a + M2_b + delta * delta * count_a * count_b / count; s_count[threadIdx.x] = count; } } } __syncthreads(); } ``` This assumes `blockDim.x` is a power of 2. I'll use 256. Now, for the second pass writing output: ```cuda float mean = s_mean[0]; float var = s_count[0] > 0 ? (s_M2[0] / s_count[0]) : 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[flat_idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } ``` Wait, I need to make sure `g` is available. In my revised design with looping, `g` is part of `idx` calculation. Let me finalize with exact code. Actually, let me reconsider whether I should use Welford's algorithm or just two-pass. Two-pass is conceptually simpler: 1. First loop: compute local_sum, reduce to mean 2. Second loop: compute local_sq_sum, reduce to var 3. Third loop: write output Three loops means three passes over memory. Welford does it in one pass for mean/var, then one pass for output: two passes total. Given the complexity of Welford merging, let me just use two-pass. For our problem sizes, three passes is probably still fine since memory bandwidth is high. ```cuda // First pass: compute sum float local_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; local_sum += val; } s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Reduce sum for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / total_elements; // Second pass: compute variance sum float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; float diff = val - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_sq_sum[0] / total_elements; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Third pass: write output for (...) { float val = input[...] + temb_val; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[...] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } ``` Simple, clear, and should be accurate. The extra memory pass is acceptable. For performance, I should use `blockDim.x = 256` and unroll where possible. But this basic version should be competitive. self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: hidden_states = cuda_module.fused_groupnorm_silu_cl( hidden_states, self.norm2.weight, self.norm2.bias, self.norm2.num_groups, self.norm2.eps ) else: if temb_input is not None: hidden_states = hidden_states + temb_input if hasattr(self, 'norm2') and self.norm2 is not None: hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'nonlinearity') and self.nonlinearity is not None: hidden_states = self.nonlinearity(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'downsample') and self.downsample is not None: input_tensor = self.downsample(input_tensor) hidden_states = self.downsample(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'dropout') and self.dropout is not None: hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.conv2(hidden_states) if hasattr(self, 'conv_shortcut') and self.conv_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.conv_shortcut(input_tensor) elif hasattr(self, 'nin_shortcut') and self.nin_shortcut is not None: input_tensor = self.nin_shortcut(input_tensor) scale = getattr(self, 'output_scale_factor', 1.0) output_tensor = (input_tensor + hidden_states) / scale return output_tensor for module in model.modules(): if isinstance(module, ResnetBlock2D): module.forward = types.MethodType(fast_forward, module) class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name): super().__init__() from diffusers import UNet2DConditionModel torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark = True self.model = UNet2DConditionModel.from_pretrained(model_name, subfolder="unet") # Set __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int n = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int spatial = H * W; int total_elements = group_size * spatial; __shared__ float s_sum[MAX_THREADS]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[MAX_THREADS]; int base_offset = n * spatial * C; // First pass: compute mean float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; local_sum += input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; } s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / total_elements; // Second pass: compute variance float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float diff = val - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_sq_sum[0] / total_elements; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Third pass: write output for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float val = input[flat_idx] + temb[n * C + c]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[flat_idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } __global__ void fused_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel( const 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = g * group_size + (idx / spatial); int hw = idx % spatial; int flat_idx = base_offset + hw * C + c; float diff = input[flat_idx] - mean; local_sq_sum += diff * diff; } s_sq_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { * inv_std; float scaled = norm * weight[c] + bias[c]; output[flat_idx] = scaled / (1.0f + expf(-scaled)); } } torch::Tensor fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor temb, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int num_groups, float eps) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); dim3 blocks(num_groups, N); int threads = MAX_THREADS; fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), temb.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, num_groups, eps); return output; } torch::Tensor fused_groupnorm_silu_cl(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int num_groups, float eps) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); dim3 blocks(num_groups, N); int threads = MAX_THREADS; fused_groupnorm_silu_cl_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, num_groups, eps); return output; } """ cuda_module = load_inline( name="unet_fused_ops", cpp_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_add_groupnorm_silu_cl", My patched forward assumes it's the GroupNorm after conv1 and time embedding. In standard ResNet blocks, this is correct. I'm ready to generate my first iteration of code. Wait, I just realized there's a potential bug in my kernel. When I do: ```cuda int base_offset = n * spatial * C; ``` This assumes the batch stride is `spatial * C`. For channels_last, the batch stride is indeed `spatial * C` because: - Logical shape: [N, C, H, W] - Strides: [C*H*W, 1, W*C, C] - stride[0] = C*H*W = spatial * C So `base_offset` is correct. For `temb[n * C + c]`, this assumes `temb` has batch stride C. For [N, C, 1, 1] with either contiguous or channels_last strides, stride[0] = C. So `n * C + c` is correct. Great. Let me also consider whether I need to handle the case where C % num_groups != 0. In PyTorch, `nn.GroupNorm` requires C % num_groups == 0. So it's guaranteed. Let me now submit for compilation.
